فر ماتے تھے کہ ایک عورت اپنے شوہر کی دیت میں وارث نہیں ہوگی ۔ پس ضحاک بن سفیان نے فاروق اعظم کو خبر دی کہ اللہ کے رسول صلى الله عليه وسلم نے میرے پاس یہ حدیث لکھ کر بھیجی کہ اشیم نہانی کی بیوی کو انکے شوہر کی دیعت میں وارث بناؤ۔ یہ اس وقت لکھ کر بھیجا تھا جب اشیم ضبابی حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کی حیات میں خطا قتل کر دیے گئے تھے ۔ اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ خط معتبر ہے اگر خط معتبر نہ ہوتا تو رسول اکرم صلى الله عليه وسلم ضحاک بن سفیان کے پاس اشیم ضبانی کی بیوی کے بارے میں خط کیوں تحریر فرماتے۔ فقہاء کا اجماع بھی اسی پر ہے البتہ حدہ دو قصاص میں یہ خط معتبر نہیں ہوتا بلکہ ایسے حقوق میں معتبر ہے جو حقوق شبہ کے باوجود ثابت ہو جاتے ہیں ۔ جمیل احمد عفی عنہ۔
Chapter: The Book of the Judge Writing to Another Judge. Translation: This chapter is about going to another judge with a letter from one judge. Kitab al-Adab al-Qadi. Explanation: The author of Hidayah mentioned this chapter after the section on imprisonment because one judge is needed for imprisonment and detention, while the chapter on the judge writing to another judge involves two judges. Thus the judge writing to another judge is like a compound and judgment with imprisonment is like a simple matter. And since the compound comes after the simple, therefore the chapter on the judge writing to another judge is mentioned later, and the section on imprisonment is mentioned first. Ibn al-Humam wrote in Fath al-Qadir that a judge acting upon another judge's letter is against analogy. Because a judge's letter cannot be greater than the judge himself giving news, whereas if a judge of one city informed another city's judge orally that 'the right for such-and-such person residing in your city has been established by witnesses who testified before me and I accepted them', then the other judge is not allowed to act upon that news. Because a judge's giving news is not proof outside his jurisdiction, so his letter is even less than that. But by the consensus of the Companions and the Successors, a letter from one judge to another is permissible, and people have need for it because a person may not be able to gather his witnesses and the defendant. For example, the witnesses are in one city and the defendant is in another city, and it is impossible for the plaintiff to bring both together. So the plaintiff produces the witnesses' testimony before the judge and takes that judge's letter to the other judge where the defendant is present. Thus the letter was made permissible so that the rightful person may get his right. And if it is objected that one writing can resemble another writing, and one seal can resemble another seal, so there is suspicion of forgery, then the answer is that this suspicion can be removed by the condition that two witnesses be present with the letter who testify that whatever is in the letter is from the judge who sent it and that he affixed his seal on it. On the permissibility of a judge's letter to another judge, the hadith of Duhak bin Sufyan is a just witness. The hadith is that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wrote to him to make the wife of Ashim al-Diyabi inherit from the blood money of her husband. The full story is that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) was of the opinion that a woman would not inherit from her husband's blood money. Then Duhak bin Sufyan informed Umar that the Messenger of Allah wrote to me: 'Inherit the wife of Ashim al-Diyabi from the blood money of her husband.' This was written when Ashim al-Diyabi was killed by mistake during the lifetime of the Prophet. From this hadith it is understood that a letter is reliable. If a letter were not reliable, why would the Prophet (peace be upon him) write a letter to Duhak bin Sufyan concerning the wife of Ashim al-Diyabi? The consensus of the jurists is also upon this. However, this letter is not reliable in matters of hadd and qisas, but is reliable in those rights which are established despite suspicion. Jamil Ahmad, may Allah forgive him.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah explaining the chapter on one judge writing to another judge (Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi). The commentator first justifies why this chapter comes after the section on imprisonment: imprisonment involves one judge, while inter-judge correspondence involves two, so the latter is like a compound matter that follows the simple. He then cites Ibn al-Humam's observation that acting on a judge's letter is against qiyas (analogy) because a judge's oral testimony holds no weight outside his own jurisdiction, so his written letter should a fortiori carry no weight. However, the ruling is established by the consensus (ijma) of the Companions and Successors, and also by practical necessity (hajat) — plaintiffs often cannot bring witnesses and defendants together from different cities. The suspicion of forgery (shubhah) is addressed by requiring two witnesses to attest to the authenticity of the letter and seal. The hadith of Duhak bin Sufyan is cited as a proof: the Prophet wrote a letter instructing that a widow inherit from her husband's blood money, demonstrating that such letters are reliable evidence. The commentary concludes by noting that this reliability applies only to financial rights, not to hadd punishments or qisas, because those require stricter proof and are not established in cases of doubt (shubhah).

Key Terms
كتاب القاضى الى القاضى (Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi): The chapter dealing with a judge's written communication to another judge regarding a case.
اجماع (ijma): Consensus of the Companions and Successors, used as a proof to override qiyas.
خلاف قياس (khilaf qiyas): Against analogical reasoning, used to highlight that the ruling is exceptional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must be met for a judge's letter to another judge to be accepted?
A) The letter must be sealed with the judge's personal seal.
B) Two witnesses must testify that the letter is from that judge and sealed by him.
C) The letter must be written in Arabic.
D) The plaintiff must accompany the letter.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter to another judge is valid proof when authenticated by two witnesses, based on consensus and Prophetic practice, but does not apply to hadd or qisas.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
The Judge's Letter: Ruling, Proof, and Overriding Qiyas
States the ruling and proof for a judge's letter to another judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge's letter to another judge, identify the proof from the hadith of Dahhak ibn Sufyan, and explain why qiyas is overridden by ijma and need.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is valid in rights despite being against qiyas, upheld by ijma and need.

This lesson examines the foundational ruling that a judge's letter to another judge is acceptable in legal rights. Although strict analogy (qiyas) would reject it—since a judge's statement is not proof outside his jurisdiction—the Hanafi school relies on the consensus of the Companions and the practical necessity of facilitating justice across cities. The Prophet's own letter to a judge provides direct precedent.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling and its exception
The Hanafi ruling: a judge's letter is accepted in rights (huquq) but not in hudud (prescribed punishments) or qisas (retaliation). Understand the scope.
2
Identify the qiyas objection
Why is this contrary to qiyas? Because a judge's oral testimony is not proof outside his jurisdiction; a written letter is even weaker. The text cites Ibn al-Humam's report of this objection.
Qiyas would reject the letter entirely.
3
Analyze the overriding evidence
The consensus (ijma) of Sahaba and Tabi'in, and the practical need (hajah) of litigants who cannot bring witnesses and defendant together, override the qiyas. The need is real and pressing.
Ijma is a stronger source than qiyas.
4
Examine the prophetic proof
The hadith of Dahhak ibn Sufyan: the Prophet (peace be upon him) wrote a letter regarding inheritance of blood money. This shows that a judge's letter carries authority, as the Prophet's writing is authoritative.
The imperative (ورثت) indicates obligation.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a plaintiff with witnesses in one city and defendant in another, the first judge writes a letter. According to Hanafi fiqh, this letter is valid and binding in the second court, provided two witnesses attest to its authenticity.

کیونکہ گواہ ، مدعی علیہ کے سامنے گواہی دیں گے یا اس کی عدم موجودگی میں گواہی دیں گے۔ اگر گواہوں نے مدنی علیہ کے سامنے گواہی دی ہے تو چونکہ شرعی حجت پائی گئی اسلئے قاضی کا تب ، شہادت کے موافق حکم دیدے گا اور اپنے اس حکم کو تحریر کرے گا کہ میں نے گواہی کے موافق فلاں مدعی علیہ پر حکم دیدیا ہے۔ اس کو مکمل کہا جاتا ہے۔ لیکن یہاں سوال ہو گا کہ جب گواہی مدنی علیہ کے سامنے دی گئی اور اس کے سامنے حکم دیا گیا تو پھر دوسرے قاضی کے پاس خط بھیجنے کی کیا ضرورت ہے؟ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ایسا ہو سکتا ہے کہ قاضی نے امل امیہ حاضر پر حکم تو دیدیا ہومگر اس کو جاری نہ کیا ہو۔ اور اس عرصہ میں مدعلی علیہ دوسرے شہر میں چلا گیا ہو تو اجرائے حکم کے لئے وہاں کے قاضی کے نام خط لکھنے کی ضرورت پڑے گی۔ اور اگر گواہوں نے خصم ( مدعی علیہ ) کی عدم موجودگی میں گواہی دی ہو تو ہمارے نزدیک چونکہ غائب کے خلاف حکم دینا جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے قاضی کا تب حکم نہیں دے گا بلکہ فقط شہادت تحریر کرے گا۔ تا کہ مکتوب الیہ قاضی اس گواہی کے موافق حکم
Chapter on the Judge's Letter to Another Judge. Translation: This chapter is about the statement of a judge taking a letter to another judge. Explanation: The author of Hidayah mentioned this chapter after the chapter on imprisonment because for imprisonment and detention one judge is needed, and for a judge's letter to another judge, two judges are needed. So the judge's letter to another judge is like a compound, and judgment with imprisonment is like a simple. Since a compound comes after a simple, therefore the judge's letter to another judge is mentioned later and the chapter on imprisonment earlier. Ibn al-Humam wrote in Fath al-Qadir that acting upon a judge's letter by another judge is contrary to analogy. Because a judge's letter cannot be more than the judge himself giving news. If a judge of one city informs another judge in person that for such and such a person in your city, this right is proven by such witnesses who testified before me and I accepted them, then acting upon that news by the other judge is not permissible. Because a judge's informing is not proof outside his jurisdiction, so his letter is even less likely to be proof. However, by the consensus of the Companions and Successors, a judge's letter to another judge is permissible, and people have need of it. Because a person sometimes cannot bring together his witnesses and the defendant. For example, the witnesses are in one city and the defendant is in another, and it is not possible for the plaintiff to gather both. So the plaintiff presents the witnesses' testimony before the judge, takes his letter, and goes to the other judge where the defendant is present. Thus this letter is made permissible so that the rightful claimant may get his right. If it is objected that one writing resembles another, and one seal resembles another, so there is suspicion of fraud, the answer is that this suspicion can be removed by the condition that with the judge's letter there be two witnesses who testify that whatever is in the letter is from that judge who sent it and he affixed his seal. On the permissibility of the judge's letter to another judge, the hadith of Dahhak ibn Sufyan is a just witness. The hadith: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wrote to him that the woman of Ashim al-Dhababi should inherit from the blood money of her husband. The full story is that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) held the opinion that a woman would not inherit from the blood money of her husband. So Dahhak ibn Sufyan informed Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) sent a letter to me that the wife of Ashim al-Dhababi should be made to inherit from the blood money of her husband. This letter was sent when Ashim al-Dhababi was killed by mistake during the lifetime of the Prophet (peace be upon him). From this hadith it is known that the letter is authoritative. If the letter were not authoritative, why would the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) write a letter to Dahhak ibn Sufyan about the wife of Ashim al-Dhababi? The jurists are also in consensus on this. However, in hudud and qisas, this letter is not authoritative; rather, it is authoritative in such rights that are proven despite doubts. Jamil Ahmad, may he be forgiven. Kitab al-Adab al-Qadi: In which rights is a judge's letter to another judge acceptable? The text: And the letter of a judge to a judge is accepted in rights, when it is testified before him out of need, as we will explain. If they testify in the presence of a litigant present, he judges by the testimony because proof exists, and he writes his judgment, which is called a sijill. If they testify in the absence of the litigant, he does not judge because judgment against an absent person is not permissible, and he writes the testimony so that the addressee judge may judge by it. This is the hukmi letter, and it is essentially a transfer of testimony. It is characterized by conditions we will mention, Allah willing. Its permissibility is due to pressing need, because the plaintiff may find it difficult to gather his witnesses and his opponent, so it resembles testimony upon testimony. Translation: And the letter of a judge to a judge will be accepted in rights when testimony of the letter is given before the second judge. This permissibility is due to need, as we will explain. So if the witnesses testify in the presence of the litigant present, then the judge will give judgment according to the testimony because the proof is found, and he will write his judgment, and that is called a sijill. And if the witnesses testify in the absence of the litigant, then the judge will not give judgment because judgment against the absent is not permissible. And the judge will write the testimony so that the addressee judge may decide according to it. This is called a hukmi letter. And it is in reality a transfer of testimony. And it is characterized by conditions which we will mention, Allah willing. And its permissibility is due to need. Explanation: Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri said: A judge's letter to another judge is accepted in such rights that are established despite doubts, provided that two witnesses testify before the addressee judge that this letter is from that judge (the sender) and that it is his seal. And in rights that are nullified due to doubts, like hudud and qisas, a judge's letter to another judge is not accepted. Then the judge's letter has two types: 1. Sijill letter, 2. Hukmi letter. Because the witnesses will either testify in the presence of the defendant or in his absence. If the witnesses testify in the presence of the defendant, then since the legal proof is found, the judge (the first) will give judgment according to the testimony and write his judgment that he has given judgment against the defendant according to the testimony. This is called a sijill. But here a question arises: when the testimony was given in the presence of the defendant and judgment was passed in his presence, what need is there to send a letter to another judge? The answer is that it is possible that the judge passed judgment on the defendant but did not execute it, and during that period the defendant went to another city, so for execution of judgment it becomes necessary to write a letter to the judge there. And if the witnesses testified in the absence of the defendant, then according to us, since giving judgment against an absent person is not permissible, the judge (the first) will not give judgment but will only write the testimony, so that the addressee judge may give judgment according to it.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the chapter on a judge writing to another judge. It begins by establishing that this chapter is placed after the chapter on imprisonment because the latter requires one judge while the former requires two, following the principle that the compound comes after the simple. The core ruling is that a letter from one judge to another is valid and binding in matters of rights (huquq), but not in hudud (prescribed punishments) or qisas (retaliation). This ruling is initially contrary to qiyas (analogy), because a judge's statement is not normally proof outside his jurisdiction. However, it is established by the ijma (consensus) of the Sahaba and Tabi'in, and by the practical need (hajah) of people who cannot easily bring witnesses and defendants together from different cities. The text provides a hadith of Dahhak ibn Sufyan as evidence: the Prophet (peace be upon him) wrote a letter regarding inheritance of blood money, proving the authority of written correspondence. The text then distinguishes two types of letters: (1) Sijill – a letter recording a judgment already passed in the presence of the defendant, used for execution when the defendant has moved elsewhere. (2) Kitab Hukmi – a letter transmitting testimony, used when the defendant is absent and the first judge cannot pass judgment (since judgment against an absentee is not allowed among the Hanafis). The letter is accepted only if two witnesses testify before the second judge that the letter is genuinely from the first judge and bears his seal. This protects against forgery. The commentary by Ibn al-Humam and al-Quduri reinforces that hudud and qisas are excluded because they are nullified by doubts, and the letter is like 'testimony upon testimony' (shahadah ala shahadah) in its transfer of evidentiary value.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi): A letter from one judge to another judge regarding a legal matter, either transmitting a judgment (sijill) or testimony (hukmi).
سجل (sijill): A letter from a judge recording a judgment that has been passed in the presence of the defendant, sent to another judge for execution.
كتاب حكمي (kitab hukmi): A letter from a judge transmitting testimony (shahadah) to another judge, when the defendant is absent, so that the second judge may pass judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Hanafi acceptance of a judge's letter to another judge, according to the text?
A) Analogy (qiyas) to oral testimony within jurisdiction.
B) Consensus (ijma) of the Companions and practical need (hajah).
C) The opinion of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani alone.
D) A single hadith with weak transmission.

Key Takeaway
The judge's letter is a valid judicial instrument in rights, based on ijma and need, overriding strict qiyas.

Qada — Judiciary

Judge's Letter Types: Sijill and Hukmi with Conditions
Explains two types of judge's letters and their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a sijill letter and a hukmi letter, state the conditions for accepting a judge's letter, and identify which rights are excluded.

Core Idea
Two types: sijill (transmits judgment) and hukmi (transmits testimony); both require two witnesses and are valid only in rights, not hudud/qisas.

This lesson deepens your understanding of the judge's letter by classifying it into two distinct types based on the presence or absence of the defendant during testimony. The sijill records a judgment already passed, while the hukmi transfers testimony for a future judgment. Both require two witnesses to attest authenticity, and both are excluded in hudud and qisas due to doubt. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying the conditions in practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types based on defendant's presence
If the defendant is present when testimony is given: the judge passes judgment and writes a sijill (a record of the judgment) for execution elsewhere. If the defendant is absent: the judge writes a hukmi letter (a record of the testimony) so that the second judge can pass judgment after hearing the defendant.
The key is whether the first judge could pass judgment or not. Since judgment against an absentee is not allowed in Hanafi fiqh, only testimony is transmitted.
2
Identify the condition of two witnesses
Both types require that two witnesses testify before the second judge that the letter is from the sending judge and bears his seal. This prevents forgery.
This condition is explicitly stated in the text as a response to the objection of forgery.
3
Apply the exclusion of hudud and qisas
The letter is not accepted in hudud (prescribed punishments) or qisas (retaliation) because these are nullified by doubts (shubuhat). Only rights that are proven despite doubts are included.
This exclusion is based on the principle that hudud and qisas are dropped by doubt.
4
Explain the analogy to shahadah ala shahadah
The hukmi letter essentially transfers testimony from one court to another, similar to testimony upon testimony (shahadah ala shahadah). This analogy helps in understanding its evidentiary nature.
The text says the hukmi letter 'resembles testimony upon testimony'.
5
Trace the practical need
The need arises when the plaintiff cannot gather witnesses and defendant in one location. The hukmi letter bridges that gap by allowing testimony to be taken in one city and acted upon in another.
This need is the same 'hajah' that justifies the entire institution.

۲ کتاب حکمی
کیونکہ گواہ ، مدعی علیہ کے سامنے گواہی دیں گے یا اس کی عدم موجودگی میں گواہی دیں گے۔ اگر گواہوں نے مدنی علیہ کے سامنے گواہی دی ہے تو چونکہ شرعی حجت پائی گئی اسلئے قاضی کا تب ، شہادت کے موافق حکم دیدے گا اور اپنے اس حکم کو تحریر کرے گا کہ میں نے گواہی کے موافق فلاں مدعی علیہ پر حکم دیدیا ہے۔ اس کو مکمل کہا جاتا ہے۔ لیکن یہاں سوال ہو گا کہ جب گواہی مدنی علیہ کے سامنے دی گئی اور اس کے سامنے حکم دیا گیا تو پھر دوسرے قاضی کے پاس خط بھیجنے کی کیا ضرورت ہے؟ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ایسا ہو سکتا ہے کہ قاضی نے امل امیہ حاضر پر حکم تو دیدیا ہومگر اس کو جاری نہ کیا ہو۔ اور اس عرصہ میں مدعلی علیہ دوسرے شہر میں چلا گیا ہو تو اجرائے حکم کے لئے وہاں کے قاضی کے نام خط لکھنے کی ضرورت پڑے گی۔ اور اگر گواہوں نے خصم ( مدعی علیہ ) کی عدم موجودگی میں گواہی دی ہو تو ہمارے نزدیک چونکہ غائب کے خلاف حکم دینا جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے قاضی کا تب حکم نہیں دے گا بلکہ فقط شہادت تحریر کرے گا۔ تا کہ مکتوب الیہ قاضی اس گواہی کے موافق حکم
Chapter on the Judge's Letter to Another Judge. Translation: This chapter is about the statement of a judge taking a letter to another judge. Explanation: The author of Hidayah mentioned this chapter after the chapter on imprisonment because for imprisonment and detention one judge is needed, and for a judge's letter to another judge, two judges are needed. So the judge's letter to another judge is like a compound, and judgment with imprisonment is like a simple. Since a compound comes after a simple, therefore the judge's letter to another judge is mentioned later and the chapter on imprisonment earlier. Ibn al-Humam wrote in Fath al-Qadir that acting upon a judge's letter by another judge is contrary to analogy. Because a judge's letter cannot be more than the judge himself giving news. If a judge of one city informs another judge in person that for such and such a person in your city, this right is proven by such witnesses who testified before me and I accepted them, then acting upon that news by the other judge is not permissible. Because a judge's informing is not proof outside his jurisdiction, so his letter is even less likely to be proof. However, by the consensus of the Companions and Successors, a judge's letter to another judge is permissible, and people have need of it. Because a person sometimes cannot bring together his witnesses and the defendant. For example, the witnesses are in one city and the defendant is in another, and it is not possible for the plaintiff to gather both. So the plaintiff presents the witnesses' testimony before the judge, takes his letter, and goes to the other judge where the defendant is present. Thus this letter is made permissible so that the rightful claimant may get his right. If it is objected that one writing resembles another, and one seal resembles another, so there is suspicion of fraud, the answer is that this suspicion can be removed by the condition that with the judge's letter there be two witnesses who testify that whatever is in the letter is from that judge who sent it and he affixed his seal. On the permissibility of the judge's letter to another judge, the hadith of Dahhak ibn Sufyan is a just witness. The hadith: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wrote to him that the woman of Ashim al-Dhababi should inherit from the blood money of her husband. The full story is that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) held the opinion that a woman would not inherit from the blood money of her husband. So Dahhak ibn Sufyan informed Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) sent a letter to me that the wife of Ashim al-Dhababi should be made to inherit from the blood money of her husband. This letter was sent when Ashim al-Dhababi was killed by mistake during the lifetime of the Prophet (peace be upon him). From this hadith it is known that the letter is authoritative. If the letter were not authoritative, why would the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) write a letter to Dahhak ibn Sufyan about the wife of Ashim al-Dhababi? The jurists are also in consensus on this. However, in hudud and qisas, this letter is not authoritative; rather, it is authoritative in such rights that are proven despite doubts. Jamil Ahmad, may he be forgiven. Kitab al-Adab al-Qadi: In which rights is a judge's letter to another judge acceptable? The text: And the letter of a judge to a judge is accepted in rights, when it is testified before him out of need, as we will explain. If they testify in the presence of a litigant present, he judges by the testimony because proof exists, and he writes his judgment, which is called a sijill. If they testify in the absence of the litigant, he does not judge because judgment against an absent person is not permissible, and he writes the testimony so that the addressee judge may judge by it. This is the hukmi letter, and it is essentially a transfer of testimony. It is characterized by conditions we will mention, Allah willing. Its permissibility is due to pressing need, because the plaintiff may find it difficult to gather his witnesses and his opponent, so it resembles testimony upon testimony. Translation: And the letter of a judge to a judge will be accepted in rights when testimony of the letter is given before the second judge. This permissibility is due to need, as we will explain. So if the witnesses testify in the presence of the litigant present, then the judge will give judgment according to the testimony because the proof is found, and he will write his judgment, and that is called a sijill. And if the witnesses testify in the absence of the litigant, then the judge will not give judgment because judgment against the absent is not permissible. And the judge will write the testimony so that the addressee judge may decide according to it. This is called a hukmi letter. And it is in reality a transfer of testimony. And it is characterized by conditions which we will mention, Allah willing. And its permissibility is due to need. Explanation: Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri said: A judge's letter to another judge is accepted in such rights that are established despite doubts, provided that two witnesses testify before the addressee judge that this letter is from that judge (the sender) and that it is his seal. And in rights that are nullified due to doubts, like hudud and qisas, a judge's letter to another judge is not accepted. Then the judge's letter has two types: 1. Sijill letter, 2. Hukmi letter. Because the witnesses will either testify in the presence of the defendant or in his absence. If the witnesses testify in the presence of the defendant, then since the legal proof is found, the judge (the first) will give judgment according to the testimony and write his judgment that he has given judgment against the defendant according to the testimony. This is called a sijill. But here a question arises: when the testimony was given in the presence of the defendant and judgment was passed in his presence, what need is there to send a letter to another judge? The answer is that it is possible that the judge passed judgment on the defendant but did not execute it, and during that period the defendant went to another city, so for execution of judgment it becomes necessary to write a letter to the judge there. And if the witnesses testified in the absence of the defendant, then according to us, since giving judgment against an absent person is not permissible, the judge (the first) will not give judgment but will only write the testimony, so that the addressee judge may give judgment according to it.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the chapter on a judge writing to another judge. It begins by establishing that this chapter is placed after the chapter on imprisonment because the latter requires one judge while the former requires two, following the principle that the compound comes after the simple. The core ruling is that a letter from one judge to another is valid and binding in matters of rights (huquq), but not in hudud (prescribed punishments) or qisas (retaliation). This ruling is initially contrary to qiyas (analogy), because a judge's statement is not normally proof outside his jurisdiction. However, it is established by the ijma (consensus) of the Sahaba and Tabi'in, and by the practical need (hajah) of people who cannot easily bring witnesses and defendants together from different cities. The text provides a hadith of Dahhak ibn Sufyan as evidence: the Prophet (peace be upon him) wrote a letter regarding inheritance of blood money, proving the authority of written correspondence. The text then distinguishes two types of letters: (1) Sijill – a letter recording a judgment already passed in the presence of the defendant, used for execution when the defendant has moved elsewhere. (2) Kitab Hukmi – a letter transmitting testimony, used when the defendant is absent and the first judge cannot pass judgment (since judgment against an absentee is not allowed among the Hanafis). The letter is accepted only if two witnesses testify before the second judge that the letter is genuinely from the first judge and bears his seal. This protects against forgery. The commentary by Ibn al-Humam and al-Quduri reinforces that hudud and qisas are excluded because they are nullified by doubts, and the letter is like 'testimony upon testimony' (shahadah ala shahadah) in its transfer of evidentiary value.

Key Terms
سجل (sijill): A letter from a judge recording a judgment that has been passed in the presence of the defendant, sent to another judge for execution.
كتاب حكمي (kitab hukmi): A letter from a judge transmitting testimony (shahadah) to another judge, when the defendant is absent, so that the second judge may pass judgment.
شهادة على الشهادة (shahadah ala al-shahadah): Testimony upon testimony; the transfer of testimony from one set of witnesses to another. The hukmi letter is compared to this concept.
حقوق (huquq): Rights (financial and civil) that are proven despite doubts, as opposed to hudud and qisas which are nullified by doubts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the defendant is absent during the testimony, what type of letter does the first judge write?
A) Sijill
B) Hukmi
C) Both types are the same
D) No letter can be written because the defendant is absent

Key Takeaway
The judge's letter has two distinct types—sijill for judgments and hukmi for testimony—each with specific conditions and procedures.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Acceptance of a Judge's Letter to Another Judge in Rights
Ruling on accepting a judge's letter with conditions and distinction between sijl and kitab hukmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting a judge's letter to another judge, distinguish between a sijl and a kitab hukmi, and identify the five conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is accepted in rights not nullified by doubts, but only the sijl is binding; the kitab hukmi is a transfer of testimony.

This passage from Kitab al-Adab al-Qadi addresses a practical problem: when a plaintiff's witnesses are in one city and the opponent in another, how can justice be served without causing undue hardship? The Hanafi school permits a judge to send a letter to another judge, but the nature of that letter determines its enforceability. We will examine the ruling, the two types of letters, the conditions that safeguard against forgery, and the proof based on necessity and analogy to testimony upon testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the opening lines: 'The letter of a judge to a judge is accepted in rights.' The act is sending a judicial letter. The ruling is permissible (ja'iz) but conditional. Note that it applies only to rights not dropped by doubts (like financial claims), not to hudud or qisas.
This is a concession (rukhsah) based on need, not a general permission.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is necessity (hajat): the difficulty of bringing together witnesses and opponent when they are in different cities. The passage also analogizes to testimony upon testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada), which is permitted to preserve rights. Recognize this as qiyas.
The Hanafi school uses necessity as a basis for permissibility, similar to other concessions.
3
Distinguish the two types of letters
Find where the text divides: if witnesses testify in the opponent's presence, the judge passes judgment and writes a sijl (record of judgment). If in absence, he writes a kitab hukmi (executive letter) transferring testimony. The difference is that a sijl contains a final judgment and is binding on the receiving judge; a kitab hukmi is only testimony and the receiving judge may use his own ijtihad.
The practical consequence: only a sijl must be enforced regardless of the second judge's opinion.
4
Identify the conditions
The text lists five things that must be known: the sending judge, the receiving judge, the subject matter, the plaintiff, and the opponent. These ensure clarity and prevent forgery. Additionally, the letter must be witnessed by two witnesses who testify to its authenticity before the second judge.
These conditions are essential for the letter to be accepted and acted upon.
5
Understand the procedure
The sample letter shows the process: the first judge writes the letter, has it witnessed and sealed, then gives it to the plaintiff. The plaintiff delivers it to the second judge, who verifies the witnesses and waits for the opponent to appear before proceeding.
This rigorous procedure protects against fraud and ensures due process.

بھی جائزہ کیا گیا ہے۔ فوائد نمونہ کے طور پر ایک خط کا مضمون فتح القدیر سے ترجمہ کے ساتھ نقل کیا جاتا ہے۔ از فلاں بن فلاں قاضی سہارنپور بنام فلاں ابن فلاں قاضی الہ آباد۔ السلام عليكم ورحمة الله و بركاته
حمد وصلوۃ کے بعد واضح ہو کہ میرے پاس ایک شخص خالد بن حامد سہارنپوری آیا اور بیان کیا کہ میرے ایک ہزار روپے شاہد بن ماجد الہ آباد کی پر قرض ہیں اور مجھ سے درخواست کی کہ میں اس کی شہادت سنکر جو میرے نزدیک درست ہو آپ کو تحریر کروں۔ پس میں نے اس سے گواہ طلب کئے تو وہ میرے سامنے فلاں فلاں گواہوں کو لایا ( گواہوں کا پورا تعارف نام و نسب اور سکونت وغیرہ تحریر کی جائے گی ) پس ان گواہوں نے میرے سامنے گواہی دی کہ خالد بن حامد سہارنپوری مدعی کا شاہد بن ماجد الہ آبادی مدعی علیہ پر ایک ہزار روپے فی الحال واجب الادا ہیں۔ میرے نزدیک یہ ثابت ہو چکا ہے کہ مدعی عالیہ پر ایک ہزار روپیہ قرضہ ہے۔ مدعی کی درخواست پر میں نے آپکو یہ خط تحریر کیا ہے۔ اور اس پر گواہ مقرر کر دیئے کہ یہ میرا خط اور میری مہر ہے۔ اور میں نے یہ خط گواہوں کو بھی پڑھ کر سنا دیا ہے۔
اس کے بعد خط لپیٹ کر اس پر اپنی مہر لگا دے پھر یہ خط مدعی کے حوالہ کرے۔ پس جب مدعی اس خط کو قاضی مکتوب الیہ کے پاس لا کر بیان کرے کہ یہ فلاں قاضی کا خط ہے تو وہ اس پر گواہ طلب کرے۔ پھر جب تک مدعی علیہ حاضر نہ ہو گواہوں کی سماعت نہ کرے۔ اور جب اس نے حاضر ہو کر اقرار کیا کہ میں ہی فلاں بن فلاں یعنی شاہد بن ماجد الہ آبادی ہوں تو قاضی مکتوب الیہ گواہوں کی سماعت
The book of the etiquette of the judge. He said: The letter of a judge to a judge is accepted in rights when it is witnessed before him due to need, as we will explain. If they (witnesses) testify in the presence of the opponent (present), he (the first judge) passes judgment according to the testimony because proof exists, and he writes his judgment—this is called a record (sijl). If they testify without the opponent being present, he does not pass judgment because judgment against an absent person is not permissible; rather, he writes the testimony so that the judge to whom it is written may pass judgment by it, and this is the executive letter (kitab hukmi). In reality, it is the transfer of testimony, and it is subject to conditions we will mention if God wills. Its permissibility is due to pressing need because the plaintiff may find it difficult to bring together his witnesses and his opponent, so it resembles testimony upon testimony. Translation: And a letter from a judge to a judge is accepted in rights provided that the letter is testified to before the second judge. This permission is due to need, as we will explain. So if the witnesses testify in the presence of the opponent, the judge then passes judgment according to the testimony because proof is established, and he writes his judgment and this is called a record (sijl). If the witnesses testify without the opponent's presence, the judge does not pass judgment because judgment against an absent person is not permissible. And the judge writes the testimony so that the judge to whom it is written may decide according to this testimony, and this is called the executive letter (kitab hukmi). This is in reality the transfer of testimony, and it is subject to conditions we will mention if God wills. Its permissibility is due to necessity because sometimes it becomes difficult for the plaintiff to bring together his witnesses and the opponent; thus it resembles testimony upon testimony. Explanation: Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri stated that a letter from one judge to another is accepted in rights that are proven despite doubts, provided that two witnesses testify before the judge to whom it is written that this letter is from that judge and bears his seal. Rights that are nullified due to doubts, such as hudud and qisas, are not accepted in a judge's letter to another judge. Then there are two types of the judge's letter: 1) record (sijl), 2) executive letter (kitab hukmi). Because witnesses may testify in the presence of the opponent or in his absence. If witnesses testify in the presence of the opponent, then since the legal proof is established, the judge passes judgment according to the testimony and writes his judgment: I have passed judgment against so-and-so according to the testimony. This is called a record (sijl). But a question arises: if testimony was given in the presence of the opponent and judgment was passed in his presence, then what need is there to send a letter to another judge? The answer is that it is possible that the judge passed judgment on the opponent present but did not execute it, and during this time the opponent went to another city, so for the execution of judgment it becomes necessary to write a letter to the judge of that place. If the witnesses testify in the absence of the opponent, then according to us, since passing judgment against an absent person is not permissible, the judge does not pass judgment but merely records the testimony so that the judge to whom it is written may pass judgment according to this testimony. This is called the executive letter (kitab hukmi). The difference between a record (sijl) and an executive letter (kitab hukmi) is that when the record reaches the judge to whom it is written, he will implement it whether it agrees with his opinion or opposes it, because it contains the judgment passed by the first judge. In the case of an executive letter, if it agrees with the opinion of the judge to whom it is written, he will implement it, otherwise not, because it does not contain the judgment of the first judge. The author of al-Hidayah stated that the executive letter is in reality the transfer of testimony; i.e., the first judge has transferred the testimony to the second judge so that the second judge may pass judgment accordingly. There are several conditions for the letter of a judge to a judge which we will mention later. Among them, it is necessary to know five things. The author of al-Inayah said: musann ma'luumatun (a known thing) ma'luumin (of a known) fii ma'luumin (for a known) lilma'luumi (for a known) 'alaa ma'luumin (against a known); i.e., the first judge must be known, the judge to whom it is written known, the thing about which the letter is written known, the person for whom the letter is written (the plaintiff) known, and the person against whom the letter is written (the opponent) known. The author of al-Hidayah said that the proof for the permissibility of the letter from a judge to a judge is need and necessity, i.e., permission was granted for the letter of a judge to a judge in view of necessity. The necessity is that sometimes it becomes difficult for the plaintiff to bring together the opponent and witnesses: the witnesses are in the plaintiff's city, the opponent in another city, the witnesses are not willing to go to the opponent's city, and the opponent is not willing to come to the witnesses' city. In such a case, there is no alternative except the letter of a judge to a judge. Hence it was permitted, and it resembles testimony upon testimony. Just as testimony upon testimony is allowed to preserve the rights of people, similarly the letter of a judge to a judge is also made permissible. As a sample benefit, the content of a letter is quoted from Fath al-Qadir with translation: From so-and-so son of so-and-so, judge of Saharanpur, to so-and-so son of so-and-so, judge of Allahabad. Peace and mercy of God and His blessings be upon you. After praise and blessings of God, let it be known that a person, Khalid bin Hamid al-Sahranpuri, came to me and stated that I have a thousand rupees owed by Shahid bin Majid al-Allahabadi as a debt, and he requested that I hear his testimony and write to you what is correct in my view. So I asked him for witnesses, and he brought before me such-and-such witnesses (their full identification, name, lineage, residence etc. should be written). They testified before me that Khalid bin Hamid al-Sahranpuri, the plaintiff, has a thousand rupees due and payable immediately from Shahid bin Majid al-Allahabadi, the opponent. It is established in my view that the plaintiff has a debt of one thousand rupees from the opponent. At the plaintiff's request, I have written this letter to you, and I have appointed witnesses that this is my letter and my seal, and I have read this letter to them and made them hear it. After that, fold the letter, put his seal on it, then hand over this letter to the plaintiff. When the plaintiff brings this letter to the judge to whom it is written and states that this is the letter of such-and-such judge, he (the second judge) will ask for witnesses. Then he will not hear the witnesses until the opponent appears. When he appears and admits that he is so-and-so (i.e., Shahid bin Majid al-Allahabadi), then the judge to whom it is written will hear the witnesses.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on the etiquette of the judge (Kitab al-Adab al-Qadi) addresses the ruling on accepting a letter transmitted from one judge to another judge (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi) in matters of rights (huquq). The passage states the ruling, provides a proof based on necessity, and distinguishes two types of letters: the record (sijl) and the executive letter (kitab hukmi). 

The ruling: A judge's letter to another judge is accepted in rights that are not nullified by doubts (i.e., financial and property rights, as opposed to hudud and qisas which are dropped by doubts). The letter must be witnessed by two witnesses before the receiving judge. The basis for permissibility is necessity (hajat/zarurat): it may become difficult for a plaintiff to bring his witnesses and the opponent together, especially if they are in different cities. This is analogous to testimony upon testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada), which is permitted for the preservation of rights. 

The passage then explains two types: (1) Sijl: When witnesses testify in the presence of the opponent, the judge passes judgment and writes a record containing the judgment. This record is sent to another judge for execution; the receiving judge must enforce it regardless of his own opinion because it contains a final judgment. (2) Kitab hukmi: When witnesses testify in the absence of the opponent, the first judge cannot pass judgment because judgment against an absent person is invalid. Instead, he writes a letter containing the testimony. The receiving judge may only implement it if it agrees with his own ijtihad, since it is not a binding judgment but a transfer of testimony. 

Conditions: The author mentions five things that must be known: the sending judge, the receiving judge, the subject matter, the plaintiff, and the opponent. These ensure clarity and prevent forgery. The passage concludes with a sample letter format from Fath al-Qadir, showing the procedure: the first judge writes the letter, seals it, and has it witnessed; the plaintiff delivers it to the second judge, who then verifies the letter's authenticity through witnesses and hears the case only after the opponent appears and identifies himself. This practical example illustrates the rigorous conditions that safeguard against abuse.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi): A written communication from one judge to another regarding a legal case, containing either a judgment (sijl) or testimony (kitab hukmi).
سجل (sijl): A record of a judgment that has already been passed in the presence of the opponent, sent to another judge for execution. The receiving judge must enforce it.
كتاب حكمي (kitab hukmi): An executive letter containing testimony transferred to another judge for judgment; not a final judgment itself.
شهادة على الشهادة (shahada 'ala al-shahada): Testimony upon testimony (indirect testimony), where witnesses testify about another's testimony. The letter from a judge to another is analogized to this.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between a sijl and a kitab hukmi in the context of a judge's letter to another judge?
A) Sijl is for hudud cases, kitab hukmi for financial rights.
B) Sijl contains a final judgment that must be enforced; kitab hukmi contains testimony and requires the receiving judge's agreement.
C) Sijl is written by the plaintiff, kitab hukmi by the judge.
D) Sijl requires two witnesses, kitab hukmi requires four.

Key Takeaway
The letter of a judge to another judge is accepted in rights not nullified by doubts; the receiving judge must enforce a sijl but has discretion over a kitab hukmi.

Conditions and Procedure of a Judge's Letter to Another Judge
Defines the judge's letter, distinguishes from sijill, lists conditions, proof, and procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions for a valid kitab al-qaadi ila al-qaadi and explain the procedure for the receiving judge.

Core Idea
A judge's letter transmits testimony, not a judgment; it is valid only when necessity prevents direct testimony and all parties are known.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on the judicial letter (khatt hukmi), a critical tool for cross-jurisdictional litigation. We will first distinguish it from the binding sijill, then examine the five conditions for its validity, the proof from necessity, and the detailed procedure the receiving judge must follow. By the end, you will be able to apply these rules to practical scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish sijill from khatt hukmi
Read the passage carefully. Note that a sijill contains a binding judgment and must be enforced by the second judge regardless of his opinion, while a khatt hukmi is merely a transmission of testimony and is not binding if it conflicts with the second judge's ijtihad.
This distinction determines whether the second judge has discretion or obligation.
2
Identify the five conditions (the five 'knowns')
From the text, extract the five conditions using the phrase from al-Inayah: the first judge, the addressed judge, the subject matter, the plaintiff, and the defendant must all be known (ma'lum). Write them down.
These conditions ensure reliability and prevent fraud.
3
Understand the proof of permissibility
The text states that the proof is necessity (hajah wa darurah) and analogy to secondary testimony (shahadat ala sh-shahadah). Reflect on why necessity justifies this practice.
Without this, many cases would be impossible to adjudicate.
4
Outline the receiving judge's procedure
List the steps: (1) Plaintiff brings the letter; (2) Judge asks for witnesses to verify the letter; (3) Wait for defendant to appear; (4) Confirm defendant's identity; (5) Hear witnesses' testimony about the letter; (6) Investigate their integrity; (7) Break the seal and read the letter in defendant's presence.
Each step protects the rights of the accused and ensures authenticity.
5
Note the binding force difference
Contrast the binding force: a sijill binds regardless of the second judge's opinion; a khatt hukmi binds only if it agrees with his own ijtihad. This is because the first judge's ruling is not contained in the letter.
This preserves the independence of the second judge.

حمد وصلوۃ کے بعد واضح ہو کہ میرے پاس ایک شخص خالد بن حامد سہارنپوری آیا اور بیان کیا کہ میرے ایک ہزار روپے شاہد بن ماجد الہ آباد کی پر قرض ہیں اور مجھ سے درخواست کی کہ میں اس کی شہادت سنکر جو میرے نزدیک درست ہو آپ کو تحریر کروں۔ پس میں نے اس سے گواہ طلب کئے تو وہ میرے سامنے فلاں فلاں گواہوں کو لایا ( گواہوں کا پورا تعارف نام و نسب اور سکونت وغیرہ تحریر کی جائے گی ) پس ان گواہوں نے میرے سامنے گواہی دی کہ خالد بن حامد سہارنپوری مدعی کا شاہد بن ماجد الہ آبادی مدعی علیہ پر ایک ہزار روپے فی الحال واجب الادا ہیں۔ میرے نزدیک یہ ثابت ہو چکا ہے کہ مدعی عالیہ پر ایک ہزار روپیہ قرضہ ہے۔ مدعی کی درخواست پر میں نے آپکو یہ خط تحریر کیا ہے۔ اور اس پر گواہ مقرر کر دیئے کہ یہ میرا خط اور میری مہر ہے۔ اور میں نے یہ خط گواہوں کو بھی پڑھ کر سنا دیا ہے۔
اس کے بعد خط لپیٹ کر اس پر اپنی مہر لگا دے پھر یہ خط مدعی کے حوالہ کرے۔ پس جب مدعی اس خط کو قاضی مکتوب الیہ کے پاس لا کر بیان کرے کہ یہ فلاں قاضی کا خط ہے تو وہ اس پر گواہ طلب کرے۔ پھر جب تک مدعی علیہ حاضر نہ ہو گواہوں کی سماعت نہ کرے۔ اور جب اس نے حاضر ہو کر اقرار کیا کہ میں ہی فلاں بن فلاں یعنی شاہد بن ماجد الہ آبادی ہوں تو قاضی مکتوب الیہ گواہوں کی سماعت کرے۔ اور اگر مدعی علیہ ، انکار کرے تو مدقی اپنے گواہوں سے ثابت کرے کہ یہی شاہد بن ماجد الہ آبادی مدعی علیہ ہے۔ میں مدعی علیہ کے ثبوت کے بعد مدعی کے گواہوں کی سماعت کرے کہ یہ خط فلاں قاضی کا ہے۔ پھر قاضی مکتوب الیہ گواہوں سے پوچھے کہ قاضی نے اس میں جو کچھ لکھا ہے وہ تم کو پڑھ کر سنایا تھا۔ اگر انھوں نے اعتراف کر لیا اور گواہی دی کہ یہ خط اس کا ہے اور اس کی مہر ہے تو قاضی مکتوب الیہ ان کی عدالت و دیانت دریافت کرے ۔ پس جب ان کی عدالت و دیانت ثابت ہو جائے تو مدعی علیہ کی موجودگی میں مہر توڑ کہ پڑھ کر سنائے۔
21. Book of Conduct of the Judge. Ashraf al-Bidaya Volume - That is called a judicial letter (khatt hukmi). The difference between a sijill (registry) and a judicial letter is that when a sijill reaches the judge to whom it is addressed, he will enforce it whether it agrees with his opinion or opposes it, because it contains the judgment given at that time by the (first) judge. In the case of a judicial letter, if it agrees with the opinion of the addressed judge, he will enforce it; otherwise not, because it does not contain the judgment of the (first) judge at that time. The author of Hidayah said that a judicial letter is essentially the name for transmitting testimony: that is, the first judge has transferred testimony to the addressed judge so that the addressed judge may give a ruling accordingly. For the kitab al-qaadi ila al-qaadi (judge's letter to another judge), there are several conditions which will be mentioned later. Among them, it is necessary to know five things. The author of Inayah said: 'from one known to a known, concerning a known, for a known, against a known' meaning: the first judge must be known, the addressed judge must be known, the matter about which the letter is must be known, the person for whom the letter is (the plaintiff) must be known, and the person against whom the letter is (the defendant) must be known. The author of Hidayah said that the proof for the permissibility of kitab al-qaadi ila al-qaadi is need and necessity: that is, permission was given due to necessity. And necessity exists because sometimes it becomes difficult for a plaintiff to gather the defendant and witnesses. For example, the witnesses are in the plaintiff's city and the defendant is in another city; the witnesses are not willing to go to the defendant's city, and the defendant is not willing to come to the witnesses' city. In such a situation, there is no alternative except kitab al-qaadi ila al-qaadi. Therefore, permission was given for kitab al-qaadi ila al-qaadi, and it becomes similar to secondary testimony (shahadat ala sh-shahadah). So just as secondary testimony was permitted for reviving the rights of people, similarly kitab al-qaadi ila al-qaadi was permitted. As a sample benefit, the content of a letter is quoted from Fath al-Qadir with translation: From so-and-so son of so-and-so judge of Saharanpur, to so-and-so son of so-and-so judge of Allahabad. Peace be upon you. After praise and salutation, it is clear that a person Khalid bin Hamid Saharanpuri came to me and stated that I have one thousand rupees as debt upon Shahid bin Majid Allahabadi, and he requested me to hear his testimony and write to you what I find correct. So I asked him for witnesses; he brought so-and-so witnesses before me (full identification of witnesses to be written). These witnesses testified before me that Khalid bin Hamid Saharanpuri, the plaintiff, has one thousand rupees immediately due from Shahid bin Majid Allahabadi, the defendant. It is established to me that the plaintiff has a debt of one thousand rupees upon the aforementioned. At the plaintiff's request, I have written this letter to you, and I have appointed witnesses that this is my letter and my seal, and I have read this letter to them and made them hear it. After this, fold the letter and affix his seal on it, then hand over this letter to the plaintiff. When the plaintiff brings this letter to the addressed judge and states that this is the letter of such and such judge, he (the addressed judge) should ask for witnesses (to testify that it is the judge's letter). Then, until the defendant appears, he should not hear the witnesses. And when the defendant appears and confesses that I am so-and-so (i.e., Shahid bin Majid Allahabadi), then the addressed judge should hear the witnesses (regarding the letter). And if the defendant denies, the plaintiff should prove with his witnesses that this is Shahid bin Majid Allahabadi, the defendant. After establishing the defendant, he should hear the plaintiff's witnesses (that this letter is from such and such judge). Then the addressed judge should ask the witnesses: Did the judge read to you what he wrote in it? If they acknowledge and testify that this letter is his and his seal, then the addressed judge should inquire into their integrity (adalah) and honesty. Once their integrity and honesty are established, he should break the seal in the presence of the defendant and read it aloud.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling of kitab al-qaadi ila al-qaadi (a judge's letter to another judge). It begins by distinguishing between a sijill and a khatt hukmi. A sijill is a written record of a judgment already pronounced; it must be enforced by the second judge regardless of his own opinion because it contains the first judge's ruling. A khatt hukmi, however, is merely a letter conveying testimony or evidence; the second judge is not bound to act if it contradicts his own ijtihad. The text then cites the author of al-Hidayah to define khatt hukmi as essentially transmitting testimony (shahadah). Next, it lists five conditions for the validity of such a letter, quoting the author of al-Inayah with a concise Arabic phrase: the first judge, the addressed judge, the subject matter, the plaintiff, and the defendant must all be known (ma'lum). The proof of permissibility is based on necessity (hajah wa darurah): when it is impracticable to bring witnesses and defendant together, the letter serves as a necessary alternative, analogous to secondary testimony (shahadat ala sh-shahadah) which is permitted to preserve rights. The passage then provides a sample letter from Fath al-Qadir, illustrating the format: it names the parties, the claim (a debt), the witnesses, and the confirmation by the first judge. Finally, it details the procedure for the addressed judge: verification of the letter's authenticity through witnesses, identification of the defendant, and reading the letter only after ascertaining the integrity of the attesting witnesses. This procedural rigour ensures that the letter's contents are reliable.

Key Terms
سجل (sijill): A written record of a judge's judgment that is sent to another judge; the second judge must enforce it.
خط حکمی (khatt hukmi): A judicial letter conveying testimony or evidence, not a binding judgment.
کتاب القاضي الی القاضي (kitab al-qaadi ila al-qaadi): A judge's letter to another judge, used to convey evidence or testimony in a case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Break the seal immediately and read the letter.

Key Takeaway
A judicial letter (khatt hukmi) transmits testimony and is enforceable by the second judge only if it aligns with his own ijtihad, unlike a sijill which is binding.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Rights Accepted via a Judge's Letter and the Dispute on Movables
Intra-Hanafi dispute on movables for judge's letter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the rights accepted via a judge's letter, explain the general rule regarding movables, and state the intra-Hanafi positions on movable chattels, identifying the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is accepted for any right identifiable by description; the later Hanafi school extends this to all movable property.

A judge's letter is a written notification from one qadi to another used to enforce judgments across jurisdictions. The Hanafi school accepts such letters for certain rights, but there is a key dispute regarding movable property. In this lesson, we examine which rights qualify and the reasoning behind the intra-Hanafi disagreement, culminating in the relied-upon position that even movables can be claimed via letter if sufficiently described.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of rights listed
List the rights explicitly mentioned in the passage: debt, marriage, lineage, usurped property, denied trust, denied mudarabah. Note the unifying principle: all are 'debt-like' in that they can be identified by description without needing physical pointing.
2
Analyze why real estate is accepted
Real estate is accepted because it is identified by specifying boundaries (tahdid), not by pointing. The letter can describe boundaries clearly.
3
Explain the general rule for movables
Movable chattels (al-ayan al-manqulah) are generally not accepted because they require physical pointing (ishara) for identification, which a letter cannot provide.
4
Detail the intra-Hanafi positions on movable property
State: (1) Abu Hanifa rejects all movables. (2) Abu Yusuf has two narrations: one accepts for male slaves only (due to ibaq), another accepts for both sexes with conditions. (3) Imam Muhammad accepts for all movables that can be transferred and changed. Note that later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) adopted Muhammad's view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify that the relied-upon position is Imam Muhammad's view, as confirmed by the statement 'wa alayhi al-muta'akhkhirun'. This means a judge's letter is accepted for any movable as long as it can be sufficiently described.

و قوله فى الحقوق يندرج تحته الدين والنكاح والنسب والمغصوب والامانة المجحودة والمضاربة المجحودة لان كل ذلك بمنزلة الدين وهو يعرف بالوصف لا يحتاج فيه الى الاشارة ويقبل العقار ايضاً لان التعريف فيه بالتحديد ولا يقبل فى الاعيان المنقولة للحاجة الى الاشارة وعن ابي يوسف انه يقبل في العبددون الامة لغلبة الاياق الأباق فيه دونها وعنه انه يقبل فيهما بشرائط تعرف في موضعه وعن محمد انه يقبل في جميع ما ينقل و يحول وعليه المتأخرون رحمهم الله

تشریح صاحب بدائیہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ جو شیخ قدوری نے کہا کہ قاضی کا خط حقوق میں قبول ہے تو اس کے تحت قرضہ، نکاح ، نسب، مغصوب ، جس امانت سے انکار کیا گیا ہو اور جس مضاربت سے انکار کیا گیا ہو سب داخل ہیں۔ کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک قرضہ کے مرتبہ ہیں ہے۔ یعنی جس طرح قرضہ ماو جب فی الذمہ کا نام ہے اور اس کی شناخت وصف سے ہو جاتی ہے اس کی طرف اشارہ کی ضرورت نہیں پڑتی۔ اسی طرح مذکورہ چیزوں کی شناخت بھی وصف سے ہو جاتی ہے۔ ان کی طرف اشارہ کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ اور قرضہ میں چونکہ خط قاضی قبول کیا جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے جو چیزیں اس کے مرتبہ میں ہیں ان میں بھی خط قاضی قبول کیا جائے گا۔ نکاح کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے ایک عورت پر نکاح کا روٹی کیا یا عورت نے مرد پر نکاح کا دعویٰ کیا۔ اور نسب کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے دعویٰ کیا کہ میں فلاں کا بیٹا ہوں یا باپ ہوں ۔ غصب کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے دعوی کیا کہ فلاں آدمی نے میرا اس قدر مال غصب کیا ہے اور امات میخوادہ کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے کہا کہ فلاں آدمی کے پاس میرے ایک ہزار روپیہ امانت کے طور پر تھے مگر وہ انکار کرتا ہے۔
And his saying: 'in rights (al-huquq),' under it are included debt, marriage, lineage, usurped property, the denied trust, and the denied mudarabah, because all of that is in the position of debt; and it is known by description and does not require pointing. And real estate (al-aqar) is also accepted because identification is by specification (tahdid). And it is not accepted in movable chattels (al-ayan al-manqulah) because they require pointing. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that it is accepted in (the case of a) male slave but not a female slave, because running away is predominant in the male slave and not in the female. And from him it is also narrated that it is accepted in both with conditions to be learnt in their place. And from Muhammad it is narrated that it is accepted in everything that can be transferred and changed; and the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) are upon this.

Translation: And under the author's statement 'in rights' are included: debt, marriage, lineage, usurped property, the trust which is denied, and the mudarabah which is denied. All are included because each is in the position of debt. And it can be recognized by description, not needing pointing. And in real estate also the judicial letter is accepted, because identification is by specifying boundaries. And in movable chattels it is not accepted because they require pointing. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that it is accepted in a male slave but not a female slave, because running away is predominant in male slaves, not in female slaves. And from Imam Abu Yusuf it is also narrated that the letter is accepted in both (male and female slaves) with certain conditions which will be known in their place. And from Imam Muhammad it is narrated that the judge's letter is accepted in all things that can be transferred and moved; and upon this are the later scholars (mashayikh al-muta'akhkhirin).

Explanation: The author of Bada'i' states: When Shaykh al-Quduri said that the judge's letter is accepted in rights, under it are included debt, marriage, lineage, usurped property, the denied trust, and the denied mudarabah. Because each of them is in the rank of debt. That is, just as debt is what is owed in the dhimmah (liability) and its identification is by description, not requiring pointing, similarly the identification of these mentioned things is by description, not requiring pointing. And since the judge's letter is accepted in debt, it will also be accepted in things that are in its rank. The case of marriage is when a man claims marriage with a woman, or a woman claims marriage with a man. The case of lineage is when a person claims that he is the son or father of so-and-so. The case of usurpation is when a person claims that so-and-so usurped such-and-such of his property. And the case of a denied trust is when a person says that so-and-so had one thousand rupees of his as a trust but he denies it.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise of the basic ruling that a judge's letter is accepted in rights (huquq). The text quotes Al-Quduri's matn and then expands. The general rule: a judge's letter is accepted for any right that is 'debt-like' (bi manzilat al-dayn), meaning it is known by description (wasf) and does not require pointing (ishara). Examples include debt (dayn), marriage (nikah), lineage (nasab), usurped property (maghsab), denied trust (amanah majhudah), and denied mudarabah (mudarabah majhudah). Real estate (aqar) is also accepted because it can be identified by specifying boundaries (tahdid). However, movable chattels (ayan manqulah) are not accepted because they require pointing, which a letter cannot provide. Then the text presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding slaves: Imam Abu Yusuf, in one narration, accepts the letter for a male slave but not a female slave, reasoning that male slaves are prone to running away (ibaq), making description less reliable; however, another narration from him accepts both with conditions. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani accepts the letter for all movable items (ma yunqalu wa yahulu), and the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) adopt his view. The commentary from Badai' al-Sanai' clarifies that the principle is that any right that can be accurately identified by description alone is eligible, while items requiring physical pointing or individual identification are not.

Key Terms
اعیان منقولة (al-ayan al-manqulah): Movable chattels that can be transported; they require physical pointing (ishara) for identification, according to the general rule.
عقار (al-aqar): Real estate; identified by specifying boundaries (tahdid), so a letter is accepted.
ایاق الاباق (ibaq): Running away of a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the late Hanafi school, for which types of property is a judge's letter accepted?
A) Only for debts and real estate.
B) Only for debts, marriage, and lineage.
C) For all movable and immovable items that can be transferred and changed.
D) For male slaves but not female slaves.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter is accepted for any right that can be identified by description, and the later Hanafi school accepts it for all movable property, overriding the earlier general rule requiring physical pointing.

Inclusion of Claims under the Judge's Letter: The Qiyas Basis
Expanding the scope of 'rights' under the judge's letter via qiyas khafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the analogy (qiyas) from debt to classify which claims are accepted under the judge's letter and identify the illa (effective cause) that governs inclusion.

Core Idea
A claim identifiable by description alone is accepted in the judge's letter, like debt; physical identification matters for movables only.

This lesson examines the scope of 'rights' (huquq) for which a judge's written testimony is accepted. The author expands the category beyond debt to include marriage, lineage, and various claims based on a common characteristic: each is a claim against the dhimma knowable by description. We will trace the qiyas from debt to these new cases and understand the distinction between rights and movables.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original case (asl) and its ruling
Debt (dayn) is the original case; the judge's letter is accepted for debt claims. This is the established rule.
2
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The common factor among the listed claims is that each is a claim against the defendant's liability (dhimmah) that can be known by verbal description without needing physical indication.
This illa is derived by tanqih al-manat.
3
List the new cases (furu')
The text explicitly lists marriage, lineage, usurped property, denied trust, and denied mudaraba as new cases.
4
Verify that each new case shares the illa
Each is a claim against liability (e.g., the right to a marriage contract, the right of ownership in usurped property) and can be described sufficiently without pointing to a specific physical object.
Objection: marriage requires identification of spouses; response: the claim is on the contract itself, not physical attributes.
5
Distinguish real property and movable goods
Real property is accepted because boundaries (tahdid) define it. Movable goods are not accepted because they require physical indication (isharah), which is impossible in a letter.
This distinction reinforces the illa: only claims knowable without physical indication are accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قوله فى الحقوق يندرج تحته الدين والنكاح والنسب والمغصوب والامانة المجحودة والمضاربة المجحودة لان كل ذلك بمنزلة الدين وهو يعرف بالوصف لا يحتاج فيه الى الاشارة ويقبل العقار ايضاً لان التعريف فيه بالتحديد ولا يقبل فى الاعيان المنقولة للحاجة الى الاشارة وعن ابي يوسف انه يقبل في العبد دون الامة لغلبة الإباق فيه دونها وعنه انه يقبل فيهما بشرائط تعرف في موضعه وعن محمد انه يقبل في جميع ما ينقل و يحول وعليه المتأخرون رحمهم الله
And his statement: 'in the rights' includes under it debt, marriage, lineage, usurped property, the denied trust, and the denied mudaraba, because all of that is in the position of debt, and it is known by description, not requiring indication. And real property is also accepted because identification therein is by boundary definition. And it is not accepted for movable goods due to the need for indication. And from Abu Yusuf: that he accepts it for a slave but not for a female slave due to the prevalence of running away in him without her. And from him: that he accepts it for both with conditions known in their place. And from Muhammad: that he accepts it for everything that is movable and transferable. And upon this are the later scholars, may Allah have mercy on them.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on the judge's letter (kitab al-qadi). It first lists what is included under 'rights' (huquq): debt, marriage, lineage, usurped property, denied trust, and denied mudaraba. The common factor is that each is a claim against the defendant's liability (dhimmah) and can be identified by description alone without physical indication. Therefore, the judge's letter is accepted, similar to a debt claim. Then it addresses real property (aqar): it is also accepted because boundaries define it. Movable goods (a'yan manqulah) are not accepted because they require physical indication for identification, which is impossible in a letter. The passage then records an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf is reported to accept the letter for a male slave (due to higher risk of absconding) but not a female slave; another report from him accepts for both under certain conditions. Muhammad al-Shaybani accepts it for all movables, and the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) follow his view. The reasoning is based on istihsan, preferring practical need over strict analogy.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written letter from one judge to another regarding a legal claim or judgment.
حقوق (huquq): Claims that are in the nature of liability (dayn) rather than specific physical items.
عقار (aqar): Real property such as land and buildings, identifiable by boundaries.
اعيان منقولة (a'yan manqulah): Movable goods that require physical indication for identification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it is a claim against the defendant's liability and can be known by description.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter is accepted for claims that are against the liability and identifiable by description, but not for movable goods requiring physical indication.

The Hanafi Dispute on Movable Goods in the Judge's Letter
Recording intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the adoption of Muhammad's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the acceptance of the judge's letter for movable goods and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The later Hanafis adopted Muhammad's view: the judge's letter is accepted for all movables, overriding Abu Hanifa's original restriction.

The earlier rule excluded movable goods from the judge's letter. However, an intra-Hanafi dispute arose. This lesson examines the views of the two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, as well as the position of the later scholars (mutakhkhirin), which now prevails in practice. Understanding this evolution demonstrates how practical need (istihsan) can lead to a broader ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the original rule
According to the base matn (Abu Hanifa's position), movable goods are not accepted in the judge's letter.
This is the default ruling from the earlier part.
2
Present Abu Yusuf's first riwaya
Abu Yusuf is reported to accept the letter for a male slave (due to prevalence of running away) but not for a female slave.
The illa is the increased need because of the habit of absconding.
3
Present Abu Yusuf's second riwaya
Another report from Abu Yusuf accepts the letter for both male and female slaves, but under specific conditions detailed elsewhere.
4
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad al-Shaybani accepts the letter for all movable and transferable goods (ma yunqalu wa yahwil).
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) adopt Muhammad's view, making it the operative position for fatwa.
This is based on istihsan and practical necessity.

Witness Requirement for a Judge's Letter
Stating that judge's letter requires two male witnesses or a man and two women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the witness requirement for a judge's letter and identify the exceptions based on the binding nature of the document.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is binding only when verified by two male witnesses or a man and two women.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes a key procedural rule: a written letter from a judge to another judge is not accepted unless it is proven by the same level of testimony as an oral claim. The author explains the reasoning—because the letter is binding—and then sets out two exceptions. Understanding this rule helps clarify how written evidence is treated in Hanafi judicial procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The rule: a judge's letter requires two male witnesses or one man and two women. This is a procedural condition for the letter to be accepted.
The condition parallels the standard testimony requirement for financial claims.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The author states: 'because writing resembles writing, so it is not established except with complete proof. And this is because it is binding.' The effective cause (illa) is the binding effect (mulzim) of the letter. A written document alone is insufficient; it must be verified by witnesses.
The reasoning applies qiyas: oral testimony is the standard, and the written letter is analogized to it due to the shared illa of bindingness.
3
Apply the exceptions
Two exceptions: (a) Kitab al-istiman from a harbi (non-Muslim) is not binding, so no witnesses needed. (b) Messages regarding character evaluation (tazkiyah) are not binding because the binding force is on the testimony itself, not the evaluation.
The exceptions are based on the absence of the illa (binding nature).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا يقبل الكتاب إلا بشهادة رجلين أو رجل وامرأتين لأن الكتاب يشبه الكتاب فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة وهذا لأنه ملزم فلا بد من الحجة بخلاف كتاب الاستيمان من أهل الحرب لأنه ليس بملزم و بخلاف رسول القاضي إلى المزكي و رسوله إلى القاضي لأن الإلزام بالشهادة لا بالتزكية
He said: The letter is not accepted except with the testimony of two men or one man and two women, because writing resembles writing, so it is not established except with complete proof. And this is because it is binding, so proof is necessary. Contrary to the letter of safe conduct from the people of war, because it is not binding. And contrary to the messenger of the judge to the one who evaluates character and his messenger to the judge, because the binding is by testimony, not by evaluation.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage states the condition for accepting a judge's letter: it must be proven by two male witnesses or one man and two women. The reasoning is that the letter resembles a written document (kitab), which cannot establish a binding ruling without full evidence. Since the letter is binding (mulzim) on the recipient judge, it requires the same evidence as a verbal testimony. Exceptions are made for letters of safe conduct (kitab al-istiman) from non-Muslims because they are not binding on the Muslim judge, and for messages (rasul) from the judge to a character evaluator (muzakki) or vice versa, because the binding force is on the testimony itself, not on the character evaluation. The condition ensures that the letter is authentic and reliable.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony given by witnesses in a legal proceeding.
كتاب الاستيمان (kitab al-istiman): A letter of safe conduct given to or received from people of war (harbi).
ملزم (mulzim): Binding; requiring the recipient to act upon it.
تزكية (tazkiyah): Character assessment or evaluation of a witness's integrity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the judge's letter not accepted without witnesses?
A) Because the letter might be forged.
B) Because writing resembles writing, and it cannot be established without full proof.
C) Because the judge must personally know the sender.
D) Because Islamic law requires written evidence for all cases.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter is only accepted after being proven by two male witnesses or one man and two women, unless it is a non-binding communication like a safe-conduct or character evaluation.
